<div id="review_1195939302" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1195939302" />
    <a title="Charles Finch" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8152701-charles-finch"><img alt="Charles Finch" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1472690312p2/8152701.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1195939302?book_show_action=true">Feb 09, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Charles Finch" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Charles Finch" href="/user/show/8152701-charles-finch">Charles Finch</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1195939302" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7490118593955530916">My USA today review.<br/><br/>*****<br/><br/>By my count I've now reviewed around 50 books for USA TODAY. I've never given any of them four stars until today: to A Spool of Blue Thread, the masterful 20th novel by Anne Tyler.<br/><br/>What makes it so good? Its subject is her most recognizable and essential one, family, its setting again Baltimore, its story told in her customarily sweet, wistful, comic voice. In other words, A Spool of Blue Thread has all the ingredients that come together to powerful effect in the author's</span>
  <span id="freeText7490118593955530916" style="display:none">My USA today review.<br /><br />*****<br /><br />By my count I've now reviewed around 50 books for USA TODAY. I've never given any of them four stars until today: to A Spool of Blue Thread, the masterful 20th novel by Anne Tyler.<br /><br />What makes it so good? Its subject is her most recognizable and essential one, family, its setting again Baltimore, its story told in her customarily sweet, wistful, comic voice. In other words, A Spool of Blue Thread has all the ingredients that come together to powerful effect in the author's best books, like Dinner at the Homesick Restaurant, but they're also the ones that can make her lesser work feel insubstantial, slight in scope, a little wan.<br /><br />What distinguishes this iteration, and places it firmly within that first class, is its risky narrative layout. For a little more than half its length, the book tells the present-day story of the Whitshanks – a classic Tyler tribe, "one of those enviable families that radiate clannishness." There are the aging parents, Red and Abby, the three loving children, and also of course the prodigal, Denny, who has the charisma of any ungraspable child. His return home seems initially to be the book's big story.<br /><br />But then, suddenly, it flies backward from the modern day. First it returns to Red and Abby in their youth, as they meet; then, going still farther back, it depicts the early Baltimore years of Red's father, a suburban Gatsby, and his obsessive construction of the beautiful house to which Denny, who never knew him, will ultimately come back.<br /><br />This structure gives A Spool of Blue Thread an infusion of intensity and energy, just when it threatens to flag. It turns out that historical fiction, which shows rather than describes the passage of time, is uniquely suited to render all that Tyler cares about – the meaning of family and memory, the desire for the preservation of what we love and the poignant consciousness that such preservation is impossible, the symbolism that exists as surely in daily life as it does in literature.<br /><br />Take the Whitshank house, for instance, which in the hands of a less subtle writer might be a clunky device. Because we see it across different eras, watching how each generation treats its lovely specificities, its chamfered edges, its double pocket doors, the house comes to feel at once real and metaphorical – as houses do in life, as families do in life.<br /><br />It's difficult to assess Tyler's place in American literary culture. On the one hand, it feels fair to compare her to domestic writers like Laurie Colwin and Adam Gopnik – beloved, limited. On the other hand, she's often too dark and querying for that comparison to work, more akin to the enigmatic Alice Munro, or, if you prefer, a direct influence on Jonathan Franzen, who also analyzes the painful recursions of family life.<br /><br />The problem is that when a writer writes the same book more than once, even if there's some slight new pressure of emphasis, it's easy to reduce their achievement to a few themes. But A Spool of Blue Thread is a flight forward – a liberation, in the way it chooses to escape the Whitshank family in order to describe them.<br /><br />Tyler, who's 73, has nothing left to prove. But she may still have new things to say.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="7490118593955530916" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1263969575?book_show_action=true">Apr 25, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sue" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sue" href="/user/show/3642045-sue">Sue</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=kindle">kindle</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=read-2015">read-2015</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=family">family</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1263969575" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15976194611551124446">A Spool of Blue Thread is the slowly unwinding story of several generations of a family living in Baltimore during much of the 20th century. This is my first experience reading Anne Tyler--which somehow seems unbelievable (though I remember enjoying the film of Accidental Tourist)--and I enjoyed it a lot.<br/><br/>Every family is it's own self, has its own identity, and that is certainly true of the Whitshanks. They seem almost a mirror--how do they compare to other families? Are they "every" family, as t</span>
  <span id="freeText15976194611551124446" style="display:none">A Spool of Blue Thread is the slowly unwinding story of several generations of a family living in Baltimore during much of the 20th century. This is my first experience reading Anne Tyler--which somehow seems unbelievable (though I remember enjoying the film of Accidental Tourist)--and I enjoyed it a lot.<br /><br />Every family is it's own self, has its own identity, and that is certainly true of the Whitshanks. They seem almost a mirror--how do they compare to other families? Are they "every" family, as they initially appear and describe themselves? Or are they a more eccentric brand of normal like you and I (if you don't object to the comparison)--only perhaps more so? And once we learn more about the earlier generation!--Well claims to "every" family seem more feeble.<br /><br />But that seems part of the point for me. Many families have odd ball members, lone wolves like Denny. The question is--how was he formed. Many have artsy, interfering mothers like Abby or single-minded fathers like Red. But the level of secrets between and among generations are what make the dynamics of this family and story so interesting for me. And Tyler writes it so well. She kept my attention.<br /><br />Tyler creates moments from life so spectacularly spot-on. There is an old saying that nothing brings out family issues like weddings and funerals. The way she deals with these is very well done--particularly the funeral. I recognized some of those moments from my own experience. <br /><br />Of course I'm left with questions at the end--not a bad thing in any novel. It indicates the author caught me in her net. Would Denny have been different if he knew less, I wonder? And who was the true Linnie?<br /><br />Oh well I'll never know but I can muse. I'm very glad I made the acquaintance of the Whitshank family and Anne Tyler. And thanks to Constant Reader for an interesting discussion.<br /><br />4 to 4.5*</span>
  <a data-text-id="15976194611551124446" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1513925002" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

  <div class="left bodycol">

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1513925002?book_show_action=true">Jan 17, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Teresa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Teresa" href="/user/show/541416-teresa">Teresa</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer1513925002" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer8249788521092495857">Though I've read all of Tyler, I wasn't hurrying to read this one for some reason. An online group discussion of it next month prompted my doing so now, and I'm glad I did. As if in a pleasurable breeze, the pages flew by, especially during Part One, which is actually the majority of the book. At points I felt like I was reading about my own family, even to the extent that we've recently gone through and are going through some of the same events. I found Abby and Red's daughters not as delineate</span>
  <span id="freeText8249788521092495857" style="display:none">Though I've read all of Tyler, I wasn't hurrying to read this one for some reason. An online group discussion of it next month prompted my doing so now, and I'm glad I did. As if in a pleasurable breeze, the pages flew by, especially during Part One, which is actually the majority of the book. At points I felt like I was reading about my own family, even to the extent that we've recently gone through and are going through some of the same events. I found Abby and Red's daughters not as delineated as their sons, but the two young women do marry men with the same first name, so perhaps that's intentional.<br /><br />Nearing the end of the aforementioned Part One, Rohinton Mistry's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/19661.Family_Matters" title="Family Matters by Rohinton Mistry" rel="nofollow noopener">Family Matters</a> popped into my head. I wondered if an Indian reader might find some of the same things in this American novel that I did while reading his so-called domestic-drama also set in a particular evolving city.<br /><br />While Part Two clears up some family mysteries for the reader (I'm not sure these qualify as secrets), I'm also not sure it added any great understanding to the family back in the future. Even without those details, I think we'd arrived at much understanding already, though I especially liked the nuanced portrait of Linnie Mae that is not at all available to the younger generation.<br /><br />As far as the title goes, I spotted a throwaway reference to it near the end of the first part, so for the rest of the book, even into the short Part Three, I kept an eye out for its reappearance. It reappears 'off-camera', which was a little bit of a disappointment, as I would've preferred it to be shown to us in the same manner Abby's painted pottery house is.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8249788521092495857" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2234857634" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2234857634" />
    <a title="Kelly" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/94602-kelly"><img alt="Kelly" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1521304176p2/94602.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2234857634?book_show_action=true">Dec 31, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kelly" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kelly" href="/user/show/94602-kelly">Kelly</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/94602-kelly?shelf=20th-century-postwar-to-late">20th-century-postwar-to-late</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/94602-kelly?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/94602-kelly?shelf=identity-crisis">identity-crisis</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/94602-kelly?shelf=woundedsoulsandfragileflowers">woundedsoulsandfragileflowers</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2234857634" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1068572493703151583">Was that... it? Honestly was that... all? Because if so, I can cease to regret as of this moment not reading any of Anne Tyler's books before. This was possibly among the most mundane books I've ever read- in the most insulting fashion I can possibly mean mundane. If I were a person who called things pedestrian, I would turn up my nose and sniff it about now. It's about the most mundane of middle class families, trudging through the practicalities and problems of that life in the most matter-of-</span>
  <span id="freeText1068572493703151583" style="display:none">Was that... it? Honestly was that... all? Because if so, I can cease to regret as of this moment not reading any of Anne Tyler's books before. This was possibly among the most mundane books I've ever read- in the most insulting fashion I can possibly mean mundane. If I were a person who called things pedestrian, I would turn up my nose and sniff it about now. It's about the most mundane of middle class families, trudging through the practicalities and problems of that life in the most matter-of-fact and predictable way. This is why I need to write more again and flex my reviewing muscles, because I'm not saying what I mean to entirely, but I have to tell you I could not have been more bored by this book. The only interesting storyline, the one I thought was meant to be the focus of the book (We Need to Talk About Denny, basically), wound up being a background thing and was never resolved. The other storylines didn't offer me anything interesting. I don't get it, are we still writing about class migration and chips on the shoulder about it, in 2017? Do we still think the suburban home and the sacrifices made for it are compelling? Particularly without people involved who make them so? I suppose the characters are very believable, and I've been into that lately. I'm all about small stakes stories, and stories about people who could exist in the real world, rather than just as literary ciphers. I am here for it. So this should have worked for me. But you still have to take those believable people and give them pathos, reveal why they shouldn't be underestimated, connect it all together in a brilliant circle. Given the title, I would have thought that Tyler knew that.<br /><br />I don't know. I'm underwhelmed. I don't like using the term middlebrow anymore*, because I think its generally condescending and used to demean books that are largely about women and domestic subjects and I think that's bullshit, but if I did, I'd use it here. I'd be saying it with different content, meant to express a certain amount of evident writing training, vocabulary, evidence of familiarity with form, and some gestures at character development. But it's so empty. I felt like I was watching a primetime network drama, and a boring one at that, but one with lovely production values. It reminded me of a bunch of shows I watched that made me trust that they were going somewhere at the start, with actors that did the absolute best they could with the material and reinforced that belief, but then turned out to be leading nowhere at all. They lead you in circles, or lead you somewhere underwhelming, or simply got lost in love for the story they were telling, without remembering that they had an audience to get invested as well. I was deeply unsatisfied by the end, but it hardly mattered because of how badly the thing had trailed off into a series of confusingly uninteresting digressions long beforehand. <br /><br />Are there better Anne Tylers? I had meant to read the generally well reviewed <i>Dinner at Homesick Restaurant</i> after this, but unless someone can make a very good case why I should, I think I'll be moving right along.<br /><br />(*-So if you see it in my reviews in the past, I'm sorry, let me know, I'll remove it, you'd be doing me a favor letting me know. Ten years of on-and-off reviewing leads to a lot of visible evolution.)</span>
  <a data-text-id="1068572493703151583" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1112623709" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1112623709" />
    <a title="Connie G" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5364077-connie-g"><img alt="Connie G" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1307502493p2/5364077.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1112623709?book_show_action=true">Aug 03, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Connie G" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Connie G" href="/user/show/5364077-connie-g">Connie G</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1112623709" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15484248448334145252">Anne Tyler writes about families and marriage so well that I always close her books thinking how "real" her characters are. "A Spool of Blue Thread" is a multi-generational story about a Baltimore family whose patriarch had roots in a poor, rural town. He started a construction company in the 1930s, built his dream home with a wide front porch, but never quite fit in with his affluent neighbors who had more privileged backgrounds. The Baltimore home almost becomes another character in the story.</span>
  <span id="freeText15484248448334145252" style="display:none">Anne Tyler writes about families and marriage so well that I always close her books thinking how "real" her characters are. "A Spool of Blue Thread" is a multi-generational story about a Baltimore family whose patriarch had roots in a poor, rural town. He started a construction company in the 1930s, built his dream home with a wide front porch, but never quite fit in with his affluent neighbors who had more privileged backgrounds. The Baltimore home almost becomes another character in the story.<br /><br />His son, Red Whitshank, married Abby and raised four children. Now in their 70s, they are having health problems and their children get involved helping them. Even the black sheep of the family returns home, and old resentments come to the surface. The Whitshanks are an ordinary family experiencing both conflict and togetherness. I enjoyed Anne Tyler's storytelling, humor, and quirky characters in this domestic tale.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15484248448334145252" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1672023974" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1672023974" />
    <a title="Vanessa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4636758-vanessa"><img alt="Vanessa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1596200358p2/4636758.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1672023974?book_show_action=true">Jun 18, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Vanessa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Vanessa" href="/user/show/4636758-vanessa">Vanessa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780099598480" href="/book/show/24385894-a-spool-of-blue-thread">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1672023974" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9843434233247862797"><b>2.5 stars.</b> <br/><br/>Hmm. I'll be honest, I was quite disappointed with this book. In fact, I was very close to DNF-ing it which is a major thing for me (someone who rarely DNFs books). After my book group discussion last night, I can definitely understand the appeal to most people, and Anne Tyler is an incredibly popular writer... but I'm not sure her style is really for me.<br/><br/>This book is essentially a family saga, that jumps between three generations of the Whitshank family and those various timelines. Th</span>
  <span id="freeText9843434233247862797" style="display:none"><b>2.5 stars.</b> <br /><br />Hmm. I'll be honest, I was quite disappointed with this book. In fact, I was very close to DNF-ing it which is a major thing for me (someone who rarely DNFs books). After my book group discussion last night, I can definitely understand the appeal to most people, and Anne Tyler is an incredibly popular writer... but I'm not sure her style is really for me.<br /><br />This book is essentially a family saga, that jumps between three generations of the Whitshank family and those various timelines. The first half of the book deals merely with the most recent generation, and the further into the novel we go, more is revealed about the family's past. Essentially, even the most seemingly normal, mundane families have their dark secrets.<br /><br />I think what made me struggle with this book a little was the fact it was almost entirely dialogue. I don't mind dialogue, but I also like description, and I felt the book was severely lacking in this. It didn't help also that a great deal of the conversations that took place were quite generic. I felt like I was watching a soap opera at times, but without the intense melodrama that comes with it. What I ended up liking a lot more were the earliest narratives (chronology-wise), that focused on how the family came to live in their big beautiful house, and how the most recent children's grandparents came together. That section was the most intriguing, and the darkest I felt. I just found the rest of it felt a little tedious to me.<br /><br />Anne Tyler is clearly excellent at capturing the feeling of a family, and showing the subtle complexities of interpersonal relationships within families. However, I like my books with a little more darkness and a little more plot, and I just didn't think this delivered on that front. Not the book's fault, but just not my preference.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9843434233247862797" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('DIKeIVXoCPrxwSyt3hyBoUSZVvUJ2sSWpBIO2OCDscfPP0wLywpCqH3oD8Gp9O7lDCiGDmxVi18w8JWgRfswtA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('mo5QXYlAZ7VSNipsvT9qbw+5hIorgf8zZ07eEcTsDXFZM4J3F6It594fCQDK1wUrRwhUcU4OsPrzrEVpYZSMAg==')})
  }

Juneau Jane, her illegitimate free-born Creole half-sister; and Hannie, Lavinia\'s former slave. Each carries private wounds and powerful secrets as they head for Texas, following dangerous roads rife with ruthless vigilantes and soldiers still fighting a war lost a decade before. For Lavinia and Juneau Jane, the journey is one of inheritance and financial desperation, but for Hannie, torn from her mother and eight siblings before slavery\'s end, the pilgrimage westward reignites an agonizing question: Could her long-lost family still be out there? <div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer4445788629772718313">Anne Tyler was born in Minneapolis, Minnesota, in 1941 and grew up in Raleigh, North Carolina. She graduated at nineteen from Duke University and went on to do graduate work in Russian studies at Columbia University. She has published 20 novels, her debut novel being <i>
  <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/112321.If_Morning_Ever_Comes' title='If Morning Ever Comes' rel='nofollow noopener'>If Morning Ever Comes</a>
</i> in (1964). Her eleventh novel, <i>
  <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/31181.Breathing_Lessons' title='Breathing Lessons' rel='nofollow noopener'>Breathing Lessons</a>
</i>, was awarded the Pulitzer Prize in 1988. She is a mem</span>
  <span id="freeText4445788629772718313" style="display:none">Anne Tyler was born in Minneapolis, Minnesota, in 1941 and grew up in Raleigh, North Carolina. She graduated at nineteen from Duke University and went on to do graduate work in Russian studies at Columbia University. She has published 20 novels, her debut novel being <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/112321.If_Morning_Ever_Comes" title="If Morning Ever Comes" rel="nofollow noopener">If Morning Ever Comes</a>
</i> in (1964). Her eleventh novel, <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/31181.Breathing_Lessons" title="Breathing Lessons" rel="nofollow noopener">Breathing Lessons</a>
</i>, was awarded the Pulitzer Prize in 1988. She is a member of the American Academy of Arts and Letters.</span>

Oh, sure, humans cause wear and tear—scuffed floors and stopped-up toilets and such—but that’s nothing compared to what happens when a house is left on its own. It’s like the heart goes out of it. It sags, it slumps, it starts to lean toward the ground.&rdquo;</span>
